In addition, predicted proteins were annotated according to KOG classification [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Koonin EV, Fedorova ND, Jackson JD, Jacobs AR, Krylov DM, Makarova KS, Mazumder R, Mekhedov SL, Nikolskaya AN, Rao BS, Rogozin IB, Smirnov S, Sorokin AV, Sverdlov AV, Vasudevan S, Wolf YI, Yin JJ, Natale DA: A comprehensive evolutionary classification of proteins encoded in complete eukaryotic genomes. Genome Biol. 2004, 5: R7-R7. 10.1186/gb-2004-5-2-r7." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-5-r39#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d53487e2055">47</a>]. All scaffolds, gene models and clusters, and annotations thereof, may be accessed at the JGI <i>Coccomyxa</i> Portal [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="The JGI Coccomyxa Portal. [&#xA;                    http://jgi.doe.gov/Coccomyxa&#xA;                    &#xA;                  ]" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-5-r39#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d53487e2061">48</a>] and can also be found in the EMBL/GenBank data libraries under accession number AGSI00000000.</p><p><i>De novo</i> identification of repeated sequences was performed by aligning the genome against itself using the BLASTN program (E-value &lt; 1e-15). Individual repeat elements were organized into families with the RECON program using default settings [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Bao Z, Eddy SR: Automated de novo identification of repeat sequence families in sequenced genomes. Genome Res. 2002, 12: 1269-1276. 10.1101/gr.88502." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-5-r39#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d53487e2069">49</a>]. RECON constructed 2,976 repetitive sequence families from 11,044 individual repeat elements or fragments. Second, identification of known repetitive sequences was performed by aligning the prototypic sequences contained in Repbase v12.10 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Jurka J, Kapitonov VV, Pavlicek A, Klonowski P, Kohany O, Walichiewicz J: Repbase Update, a database of eukaryotic repetitive elements. Cytogenet Genome Res. 2005, 110: 462-467. 10.1159/000084979." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-5-r39#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d53487e2072">50</a>] using TBLASTX. The results of the two methods were combined.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Protein families</h3><p>Annotated proteins of nine sequenced chlorophyte algae (C-169, <i>C. variabilis</i> NC64A, <i>C. reinhardtii</i>, <i>V. carteri</i>, <i>M. pusilla</i> CCMP1545, <i>Micromonas</i> sp. RCC299, <i>Ostreococcus</i> sp. RCC809, <i>O. lucimarinus</i> and <i>O. tauri</i>) were organized into 23,507 families based on shared sequence similarity (BLASTP, E-value &lt; 1e-5) using the Tribe-MCL program [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Enright AJ, Van Dongen S, Ouzounis CA: An efficient algorithm for large-scale detection of protein families. Nucleic Acids Res. 2002, 30: 1575-1584. 10.1093/nar/30.7.1575." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-5-r39#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d53487e2108">51</a>] with default parameters except inflation parameter set to 1.4. Of those, 6,326 families contained at least one <i>Coccomyxa</i> protein, including 1,851 protein families that were found in all 9 species and represent the core protein family set of chlorophyte plants. There were 2,214 protein families containing 2,305 predicted C-169 gene products with no detectable homolog in the other sequenced chlorophytes. Of these, 196 families contained 293 proteins that had significant matches (BLASTP E-value &lt; 1e-5) to other organisms (Table S6 in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-5-r39#MOESM2">2</a>). Phylogenetic relationships and potential horizontal gene transfer for these 293 proteins were further assessed using the BLAST-EXPLORER program [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Dereeper A, Audic S, Claverie J-M, Blanc G: BLAST-EXPLORER helps you building datasets for phylogenetic analysis. BMC Evol Biol. 2010, 10: 8-10.1186/1471-2148-10-8." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-5-r39#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d53487e2118">52</a>], which combines a BLAST search with a suite of tools that allows interactive, phylogenetic-oriented exploration of the BLAST results.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Phylogenetic analyses</h3><p>Most phylogenetic analyses were performed through the phylogeny.fr web platform [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Dereeper A, Guignon V, Blanc G, Audic S, Buffet S, Chevenet F, Dufayard J-F, Guindon S, Lefort V, Lescot M, Claverie J-M, Gascuel O: Phylogeny.fr: robust phylogenetic analysis for the non-specialist. Nucleic Acids Res. 2008, 36: W465-469. 10.1093/nar/gkn180." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-5-r39#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d53487e2130">53</a>]. The Phylogeny.fr pipeline was set up as follows: homologous sequences were aligned with the MUSCLE program [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Edgar RC: MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 2004, 32: 1792-1797. 10.1093/nar/gkh340." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-5-r39#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d53487e2133">54</a>]; poorly aligned positions were removed from the multiple-alignment using the GBLOCK program [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Castresana J: Selection of conserved blocks from multiple alignments for their use in phylogenetic analysis. Mol Biol Evol. 2000, 17: 540-552. 10.1093/oxfordjournals.molbev.a026334." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-5-r39#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d53487e2136">55</a>]. The cleaned multiple alignment was then passed on to the PHYML program [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Guindon S, Gascuel O: A simple, fast, and accurate algorithm to estimate large phylogenies by maximum likelihood. Systematic Biol. 2003, 52: 696-704. 10.1080/10635150390235520." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-5-r39#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d53487e2139">56</a>] for phylogenetic reconstruction using the maximum likelihood criterion. Selection of the best fitting substitution model was performed using the ModelTest program for nucleotide sequences [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Posada D, Crandall KA: MODELTEST: testing the model of DNA substitution. Bioinformatics. 1998, 14: 817-818. 10.1093/bioinformatics/14.9.817." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-5-r39#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d53487e2142">57</a>] and ProtTest for amino acid sequences [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Abascal F, Zardoya R, Posada D: ProtTest: selection of best-fit models of protein evolution. Bioinformatics. 2005, 21: 2104-2105. 10.1093/bioinformatics/bti263." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-5-r39#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d53487e2146">58</a>]. PhyML was run with the approximate likelihood ratio test (aLRT), a statistical test of branch support [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Anisimova M, Gascuel O: Approximate likelihood-ratio test for branches: a fast, accurate, and powerful alternative. Systematic Biol. 2006, 55: 539-552. 10.1080/10635150600755453." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-5-r39#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d53487e2149">59</a>]. This test is based on an approximation of the standard likelihood ratio test, and is much faster to compute than the usual bootstrap procedure while branch supports are generally highly correlated between the two methods.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Synteny and colinearity</h3><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec22">Pairwise scaffold synteny</h4><p>We identified 5,232 putative orthologous gene pairs between C-169 and <i>C. variabilis</i> using the reciprocal best blast hit criterion. In Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-5-r39#Fig2">2a</a>, the statistical significance of the number of orthologous genes shared between pairs of scaffolds was estimated by comparison with a non-syntenic model using Z-score statistics. This non-syntenic model was constructed from 1,000 randomized datasets in which the 5,232 orthologous gene pairs were reassociated at random. The number of orthologous genes in each scaffold was kept constant across replicates. For each pair of scaffolds, we calculated the mean and standard deviation of the number of shared orthologous genes in the 100 random replicates. The Z-score was determined by subtracting the mean number of orthologous genes in the non-syntenic model from the observed number of orthologous genes in the real dataset and then dividing the difference by the standard deviation. A Z-score &gt; 3 indicates that the observed number of orthologous genes is significantly higher than in the non-syntenic model with a <i>P</i>-value &lt; 0.01.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec23">Synteny correlation</h4><p>In Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-5-r39#Fig2">2b</a>, the measure of synteny correlation established by Housworth and Postlethwait [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Housworth EA, Postlethwait J: Measures of synteny conservation between species pairs. Genetics. 2002, 162: 441-448." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2012-13-5-r39#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d53487e2184">17</a>] is given by:</p><div id="Equ1" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><math xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" display="block"><mml:mrow>

                            <mml:mo class="MathClass-rel">=</mml:mo>
                            <mml:mrow>
                              <mml:mo class="MathClass-open">(</mml:mo>
                              <mml:mrow>
                                <mml:msub>
                                  <mml:mrow>
                                    <mml:mi>n</mml:mi>
                                  </mml:mrow>
                                  <mml:mrow>
                                    <mml:mi>.</mml:mi>
                                    <mml:mo class="MathClass-punc">,</mml:mo>
                                    <mml:mi>j</mml:mi>
                                  </mml:mrow>
                                </mml:msub>
                                <mml:mspace width="1em" class="quad"></mml:mspace>
                                <mml:msub>
                                  <mml:mrow>
                                    <mml:mi>n</mml:mi>
                                  </mml:mrow>
                                  <mml:mrow>
                                    <mml:mi>i</mml:mi>
                                    <mml:mo class="MathClass-punc">,</mml:mo>
                                    <mml:mi>.</mml:mi>
                                  </mml:mrow>
                                </mml:msub>
                              </mml:mrow>
                              <mml:mo class="MathClass-close">)</mml:mo>
                            </mml:mrow>
                            <mml:mo class="MathClass-bin">/</mml:mo>
                            <mml:mi>n</mml:mi>
                          </mml:mrow></math></div></div><p>where <i>n</i><sub>
                      <i>i,.</i>
                    </sub> is the row total of the number of genes on species A scaffold <i>i</i> with an ortholog anywhere in species B's genome, <i>n</i><sub>
                      <i>.,j</i>
                    </sub> is the column total of the number of genes on species B scaffold <i>j</i> with an ortholog anywhere in A's genome and <i>n</i> is the total number of orthologous genes mapped between the two species.</p><p>For each pair of genomes, the mean and standard deviation of the synteny correlation in a non-syntenic model was calculated from 1,000 randomized datasets in which the orthologous gene pairs were re-associated at random. These parameters were used to assess the significance of the synteny correlation observed in the real data by means of the Z-score statistics.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec24">Conserved adjacent gene pairs</h3><p>For each pair of genomes, the non-syntenic model was constructed by reshuffling the order of all genes (that is, orthologs and non-orthologs) in one of the two genomes, keeping the number of genes in each scaffold constant across replicates. We used 1,000 randomized datasets to estimate the mean and standard deviation of the number of conserved adjacent gene pairs in the non-syntenic model. Z-score statistics was used to assess the significance of the observed number of conserved adjacent orthologous gene pairs in the read dataset relative to the number expected by chance in the non-syntenic model.</p></div></div></section>

GB, IA, TP, JG, AK, AB, IL, EL, SL, JP, AS, AL, MB, DD and JS performed research and analyzed data. GB, DW, TY, JMC, IG, and JVE designed research. All authors have read and approved the manuscript for publication.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec25" data-title="Electronic supplementary material"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec25-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec25">Electronic supplementary material</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec25-content"><div data-test="supplementary-info"><div id="figshareContainer" class="c-article-figshare-container" data-test="figshare-container"></div>
